If this is any help - here are some tips I picked up from another person using a hot air brush -  first I let my hair air dry for 15-20 min, then use a bit of the Bumble and Bumble Thickening Spray (just on roots) or Paul Mitchell Thicken Up, then I part my hair just under the ears and clip it up on to of my head and blow out that lower area, then (with my fingers) section my hair at about the temple point, clip that up, then go around and blow out that level, then just finish with the top, starting at the back of your head and finishing with the very front.  You can hold it rolled up longer to create a flip going up or a flip going under - when you do that after having it on the warm setting, switch it to cool to set the wave.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Anyway, this is a great brush and I love using it every time I want to blow out my fine, curly hair.</li>

I use on high and it doesn&#x27;t get too hot.  Also, it does not tangle like another smaller heated curling brush that I once had.  Hair seems to glide through this brush.     <li> ...and I like them both!  They are just different.  I will try to describe those differences here to help people who are trying to decide between the two.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Conair SpinBrush:  I have had this one for about a year now, and once I figured out the rotation directions (after only a few uses) it&#x27;s a breeze to use.  I have never had it get tangled in my hair, but I also do not use the spinning feature constantly since my hair is fine textured and that would just result in static, frizz, and probably some breakage.  I mainly use the spin feature to &amp;#34;grab&amp;#34; onto my hair, then let it sit for a few seconds to create shape and volume, then spin the opposite direction to release.  I usually only use the low setting, and it gets plenty warm enough to do what I need without being overly hot.  I don&#x27;t find the unit heavy or uncomfortable to use, though I only use it to finish shaping my hair when it&#x27;s mostly (90%) dry.  It&#x27;s a 2&amp;#34; brush so it is larger than my new 1.5&amp;#34; John Frieda Hot Air Brush, but it worked great on my medium-long hair.  However, I recently cut my hair back into a chin-length bob, so it&#x27;s a little too large to work on my shorter hair for right now.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;John Frieda Hot Air Brush:  I purchased this one to try with my shorter haircut and have had it about a week, so here are my initial reactions in comparison to the SpinBrush:  (1) I don&#x27;t miss the spinning because I don&#x27;t find it necessary -- the bristles of this brush &amp;#34;grab&amp;#34; my hair just fine without it, and (2) it is definitely hotter, since I have only used the low setting on it as well.  Due to the heat and smaller size, this one seems to make a more defined curl at the bottom of my hair, and since I can get it closer to the roots I get more volume there as well.  In fact, the first time I tried it I made my hair HUGE!&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;So overall, based on my experience with both products I would recommend them for fine to medium textured hair, and definitely one of the JF brushes if your hair is above your shoulders.  Those with longer, thicker hair may be able to take more advantage of the continuous spinning feature of the Conair, but I can&#x27;t really speak to that.  The Conair&#x27;s bristles are a bit softer so it needs its spinning feature to really get ahold of the hair, while the JF brush is more like a regular round brush.  They are both great products for those of us who have never bothered mastering the round brush blowout technique!</li>

    <li> I read a ton of reviews before buying this because I definitely wanted a hot air brush and wanted to get the right one. Like others have mentioned, it would be tough to tangle your hair in it.  I was worried that it wouldn&#x27;t be hot enough, but it was.  The heat is instant and it is as hot as my hair dryer, but not super hot, which was good since I&#x27;m trying to minimize damage.  (If you want a super hot air brush, Sally&#x27;s Beauty has a popular one.)  The slide switch gives you 4 options - heat/low, heat/high, cool/high and off.  The switch isn&#x27;t perfect, but it is flat so you don&#x27;t bump it accidentally, I&#x27;m sure that was the point of the design.  My hair normally dries quickly - probably 7-9 minutes with a blow dryer.  I did my whole head from towel dry the first time to see how long it would take and it took 15 minutes all in.  Drying from root to tip and then rolling my hair with the brush gave it body and model-like bounce.  I pinned it up, did my makeup, then let it all down.  I have to say, I got some real head turns that evening.  Most of the time I&#x27;ll probably use this after I either air dry for awhile or use a hair dryer to get most of the wet out first.  I still needed some leave-in conditioner or shine product (you can do this before or after) and if you use this for more of a straightlook, you may still want to run a flat iron on the ends, but I&#x27;m very satisfied based on my expectations for this item.  BTW, I don&#x27;t work for the company (I always wonder when I read a glowing review.)</li>
    <li> Just like any new/different appliance, you will need to give yourself time to learn how to use this; once you learn the techniques you&#x27;ll love it!  I was pretty disappointed the first time I used it, but discovered the best way to use it (for me, at least).  Most important?  FOLLOW THE DIRECTIONS and wait until your hair is mostly (80%) dry - seriously.  This thing is not very powerful; if your hair is mostly wet you&#x27;ll be wasting your time.  After towel-drying, comb through then do whatever else you need to do - drink your coffee, check your email, apply your makeup, etc.  Don&#x27;t leave your hair wrapped up in the towel, it will take longer to air dry.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;When you&#x27;re ready, put your index fingers at the top of your ears and slide them straight back, horizontally under your hair to the back of your head until your fingers meet; put all of the hair above your fingers into a clip at the top of your head.  Focus only on the hair that&#x27;s left hanging.  Grab a section of it, turn the brush on to &amp;#34;high&amp;#34; and glide slowly through your hair (either above or below your hair, depending on the look you want) from roots to about an inch or two from the ends (don&#x27;t brush through the ends!).  While the hair is still in the brush, hold that hank of hair with your other hand and repeat this step until that length of hair is COMPLETELY dry, maybe 3-4 slow passes.  On the final pass-through, continue all the day through the ends, curling the brush very slowly.  Continue these steps on all of the hair that&#x27;s hanging down.  When it is completely dry, remove the clip, put your index fingers at your eyebrows, slide to the back of your head and clip up that hair.  Then follow the same steps as above to dry the hair that&#x27;s now hanging down.  Once you have done this to your entire head, keep running the brush through your hair (I&#x27;ve always left it on &amp;#34;high&amp;#34;, so I don&#x27;t know if &amp;#34;low&amp;#34; or &amp;#34;cool&amp;#34; will work differently) until it is COMPLETELY dry - that is very important.  As you work your way up your head, it will feel like each section of hair is dry, but when you think you&#x27;re all done, run your fingers through your hair - odds are it will still be very slightly damp under the thickest parts.  Just keep brushing as if you were using a regular brush.  If your hair is not completely dry, it will get frizzy.  When I&#x27;m done, I put 2 small squirts of Frizz Ease Hair Syrum in my palms, rub them together, then run my hands down the entire length of my hair, crown to tips, focusing mostly on the ends and the areas where my layers stop.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I spend about 5 more minutes using this dryer as opposed to my old hairdryer.  After having kids, my normally stick-straight hair became wavy and frizzy; the only way to control it was to blowdry and use a curling iron.  I don&#x27;t have to use a curling iron after I&#x27;m done using this brush (I probably still would for special occasions though), and I&#x27;ve cut the time I spend on my hair every morning in half.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Some reviewers had problems, saying the brush quit working not long after purchase.  There is a 3-year warranty on it, so I did make sure I saved my receipt.  The only &amp;#34;con&amp;#34; I&#x27;ve found is that it&#x27;s really easy to accidentally slide the switch from &amp;#34;high&amp;#34; to &amp;#34;cool&amp;#34; as you&#x27;re working, and if you&#x27;re not paying close attention you might not notice it right away.  The switch needs to be a little tighter so that this doesn&#x27;t happen so often.  Oh, and the coupon for Frizz-Ease Shampoo and Conditioner that was included in the box was already expired :( Other than that, I absolutely LOVE this brush.</li>

    <li> In the past, I haven&#x27;t liked these roller brush dryers because they took forever to dry and style my thick hair. The Conair just couldn&#x27;t get through my hair----&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I did a (literal) side by side comparison of the  [[ASIN:B004INUWX0 Infinitipro Spin Air Rotating Styler, Black]] and the [[ASIN:B0056GDG90 John Frieda JFHA5 Hot Air Brush, 1.5 inch]]. Let&#x27;s just say after 30 minutes or so on one side with the Infinitipro brush, I gave up. That was even on the side that I typically find easier to style. I did all that in the time it took me to get less than mediocre results with the one side of the Infinitipro brush. Anywho, the overall summary of the John Frieda device:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Pros:&lt;br /&gt;- The hair dryer actually does a decent job of drying while it styles&lt;br /&gt;- The bristles are sturdy and add shine but also allows for more precision in defining layers and getting the curl right where you want it&lt;br /&gt;- No tangles!&lt;br /&gt;- Controls frizz and makes it easy to avoid the weird waves and curls that occur near the roots with traditional hair drying. Even easy to get the back of your head!&lt;br /&gt;- Can easily maneuver different sections of hair without having to put the brush down. Although this sounds really basic and a bit silly, I didn&#x27;t appreciate it until I tried the Conair rotating brush where I had to put it down then very carefully section out hair so random hairs wouldn&#x27;t get caught in the spinning frenzy.&lt;br /&gt;- I have long, naturally wavy hair that is fine but there&#x27;s lots of it. Unlike most tools, brushes, etc. the JF brush had no problem managing the layers and truly making it look like the beautiful end curling that you get in a true blow out. I went back and fixed the frizzy mess the Conair did to my hair with the JF brush.&lt;br /&gt;- Light and easy to maneuver.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons:&lt;br /&gt;- The tip on the top gets very hot. Although I don&#x27;t need it often, it would be nice if this was some sort of heat resistant or silicon coated topper so when I need to hold it for certain angles, I can.&lt;br /&gt;- I wish the actual brush area was a little bit longer- just a smidge. It does the job though&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Why the John Frieda brush is better than the Conair Infinitipro rotating brush:&lt;br /&gt;- Easy to handle, easy to maneuver, and fits in your hand. The Infinitipro is clunky, large, and more often than not, I found myself hitting the wrong darn button and creating a crazy unexpected rat&#x27;s nest&lt;br /&gt;- It&#x27;s pretty darn fast whereas the Conair took FOREVER and didn&#x27;t even do a good job. Like I said above, I used the JF to fix the frizz &amp; limp looking curls caused by the Conair&lt;br /&gt;- Brush bristles are stiffer but still do not tangle. The more rigid bristles allow for better defined curls &amp; layers as well as more control over the angles and getting the smooth, straight look of a blow out&lt;br /&gt;- Although the hair dryer probably won&#x27;t replace the power of your typical hair dryer, the John Frieda is leaps and bounds better than the Conair. The JF brush took care of that in a few minutes.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;All in all, don&#x27;t waste your time wondering about the Infinitipro, just go with the John Frieda brush.
